Inheritance of a Disbeliever Through Multiple Ties: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on inheritance of a disbeliever through multiple ties with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a disbeliever inheriting through both lineage and cause, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i argument.

Core Idea
A disbeliever inherits through both ties unless one excludes the other, reflecting the Hanafi view of full legal capacity.

This lesson examines a nuanced fara'id rule: how a disbeliever inherits when connected to the deceased through multiple relationships. The Hanafi school allows inheritance through both ties if no exclusion occurs, while Malik and Shafi'i restrict to the strongest tie. We'll explore the reasoning and the practical difference through a clear example.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule when a disbeliever has two ties of relationship to the deceased (e.g., both lineage and marriage)? According to the passage, he inherits through both if no exclusion would occur; otherwise the stronger tie excludes the weaker.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school(s) disagree and what do they hold? Malik and Shafi'i follow a report from Ibn Mas'ud and Zayd that the disbeliever inherits only through the most confirmed (strongest) tie.
The dissenting view is based on a qawl sahabi.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the evidence for the opposing view? The report from Ibn Mas'ud and Zayd ibn Thabit is cited, but the Hanafi school maintains the first view as correct, supported by the generality of the Companions.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of the difference? Use the example of a daughter who is also a granddaughter: Hanafi gives two shares (half + sixth = two-thirds), while the opposing view would give only one share (either as daughter or granddaughter).
The thamarah is the actual distribution of the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكافر يرث إلخ : أي الكافر يرث بالنسب والسبب كالمسلم، كالبنوة والسبب كالزوجية إذا كانت غير محرمة؛ لأنه مختار مكلف فيملك بالأسباب الموضوعة للملك كالمسلم، ولأنه بعقد الذمة التحق بالمثل? Actually the OCR has a small corruption: "بالمسلم في المعاملة، فيملك بالأسباب الموضوعة كالمسلم فيكون حكمه في ذلك كحكم المسلم. (عيني) ولو حجب أحدهما: أي لو حجب أحد السببين أي إحدى القرابتين الأخرى بالحاجب، أراد أنه إذا اجتمع في الكافر قرابتان لو تفرقتا في شخصين حجب أحدهما الآخر يرث بالحاجب، وإن لم يحجب يرث بالقرابتين كما إذا تزوج أمه، فولدت له ابنا فهذا الولد ابنها وابن ابنها، فيرث منها إذا ماتت على أنه ابن، ولا يرث على أنه ابن الابن؛ لأن ابن الابن يحجب بالابن ولو ولدت له بنتا مكان الابن ترث الثلثين النصف على أنها بنت، والسدس على أنها بنت الابن تكملة للثلثين وترث من أبيها على أنها بنت ولا ترث على أنها أخت من أم؛ لأن الأخت تسقط بالبنت وهو قول عامة الصحابة الله وبه أخذ أصحابنا، وفي رواية عن ابن مسعود وزيد بن ثابت ما أن الكافر يرث بأثبت القرابتين أي بأقواهما وبه أخذ مالك والشافعي، والصحيح الأول. [رمز الحقائق: ٥٢٠/٢]
And the disbeliever inherits: i.e., the disbeliever inherits through lineage (nasab) and cause (sabab) like a Muslim, such as sonship (banūwah) and cause like marriage if it is not forbidden, because he is a free agent, responsible, and thus owns through the causes established for ownership like a Muslim, and because through the contract of dhimmah he has joined the Muslims in transactions, so he owns through the causes established for ownership like a Muslim, so his ruling in that is like the ruling of a Muslim. (cAynī) And if one of the two is excluded: meaning if one of the two causes (i.e., one of the two ties of relationship) excludes the other by the excluding one, he intends that when two ties of relationship come together in a disbeliever, such that if they were separated into two persons one would exclude the other, then he inherits through the excluding tie. And if there is no exclusion, he inherits through both ties. The example is if he marries his mother, and she gives birth to a son for him; this son is her son and also her grandson (son's son). So he inherits from her when she dies as a son, and he does not inherit as a grandson because the grandson is excluded by the son. And if she gives birth to a daughter instead of a son, she inherits two-thirds: half as a daughter, and one-sixth as a granddaughter to complete the two-thirds. And she inherits from her father as a daughter, and she does not inherit as a sister from the mother because the sister is dropped by the daughter. This is the saying of the generality of the Companions (raḍiya Allāhu ʿanhum), and our scholars (aṣḥābunā) have taken it. And in a report from Ibn Masʿūd and Zayd ibn Thābit that the disbeliever inherits through the more confirmed of the two ties, i.e., the stronger, and Mālik and al-Shāfiʿī have taken that. But the correct view is the first. (Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq 2:520)
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage addresses the inheritance rights of a disbeliever. It first establishes that a disbeliever inherits through both lineage and cause (like marriage) just like a Muslim, because he has legal capacity and ownership. The contract of dhimmah makes him like a Muslim in transactions. Then it discusses a scenario where a disbeliever has two ties to the deceased (e.g., he is both a son and a grandson). According to the Hanafi school (and the majority of Companions), if one tie would normally exclude the other, the person inherits through the stronger tie that excludes the weaker. If there is no exclusion (e.g., both ties are such that they would not exclude each other), then he inherits through both ties, receiving shares from each. An example: a man marries his mother (which is forbidden but done by disbelievers before Islam) and has a son. That son is both her son and her grandson. He inherits from her only as a son (the stronger tie), not as a grandson (which is weaker). If a daughter is born, she inherits half as a daughter and one-sixth as a granddaughter (total two-thirds), but does not inherit as a sister because the sister is excluded by the daughter. This is the Hanafi position. A dissenting view from Ibn Masʿūd and Zayd ibn Thābit, adopted by Mālik and al-Shāfiʿī, holds that the disbeliever inherits through the most confirmed (stronger) tie only, not through both. But the Hanafi view is considered correct.

Key Terms
النسب والسبب (al-nasab wa al-sabab): Lineage (blood relationship) and cause (e.g., marriage) as bases for inheritance.
بعقد الذمة (biʿaqd al-dhimmah): The contract of protection that allows a non-Muslim subject to live in Muslim territory with rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a disbeliever is both the son and the grandson of the deceased (through incestuous marriage), he inherits as:
A) Both son and grandson, receiving double share
B) Only as son, because the grandson is excluded by the son
C) Only as grandson, because that is the stronger tie
D) He does not inherit due to the incest

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats a disbeliever like a Muslim in inheritance capacity, allowing inheritance through multiple ties as long as they are not mutually exclusive due to hajb.
